Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken in two parts together with the bones above, or one of those having some cartilage, because there are two bones in it, and the part on the outside is cartilaginous, and it does not break but rather becomes bruised and twisted and dislocated.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should introduce the small finger into the nostril, and bind that fracture inside with the tied bandage, and outside with the bandage in front, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and beware that you do not cause harm to the patient during your operation.
And if the break is in the uppermost part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then you should cut at the extremity of the thread on which the dressing is applied.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you make the likeness of that.
And try to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then apply the vinegar or ten of them to the rest of the abscess's cauterization.
And insert into the extremity of the nose a thread of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one piece, or put two small tubes, if the fracture is in two pieces, and let it be in the tube the thickness of as much quantity that fills the hole of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the tubes should be wrapped in leather, and should be changed every day, but it is not seen by me; however I have seen that the thread should be wrapped in a plaster made with powdered mummy; and then lay the tube so that the bone is held firm and the cartilage is hardened.
And some wadded things are inserted into the nose, in place of a tube, that is to say a cotton plug wrapped in linen cloth; and it should be that you have the fracture of the nose with vehement movement and that the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary to you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do tubes.
And if there's an accidental wound with a postulated abscess, apply a plaster to the nose with wax or a cloth soaked in warm oil and rosewater, or on the opposite side.
But if it doesn't develop into an abscessed wound, then it is advisable to plaster the aforementioned area with flour of chickpeas and powder of dried roses mixed with egg whites; and apply this on the ulcer, and do not put anything on the nose for any reason.
But if the nasal bones are fractured with a small break or are comminuted, then it is advisable to cut into those, and extract those with an instrument suitable for that purpose; and sew the gash, and cure it with something that nourishes and consolidates, and apply plasters suited to that.
And if there's an accidental wound inside the nose, then it is advisable to cure that with warm poultices and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is fractured.
When the jaw is broken and the break is not with a wound, consider then if the fracture is only superficial, and not broken into two pieces, and curved to the inner partitions, then its treatment is easier.
And then, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and likewise if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the right index finger; and press with that finger the curvature of the fracture gently to facilitate the separation of the parts, with one hand on the outer part of the jaw and the other hand on the inner part of the fractured bone; and decently move that one.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary that you administer extension to both parts according to rectitude so that their alignment becomes possible.
And if there is a conjunction of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those with which you expect to remain with thread of gold or silver or silk; and place a bandage over the broken jaw, and a doubled cloth over it, and a large stellar bandage decently made, or a single bandage along the length of the jaw; and bind that above according to what seems suitable for the binding and what is convenient for the application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and calmness, and place his diet of soothing foods.
And if you think that the shape has already been altered in some way, then take care to dissolve it on the third day; and then rectify what has been altered in them and apply a plaster made with powder of mola, or white of egg, or flour of cypress, since you will have applied the cerate of them, and place the woolen cloth over the plaster.
And then put and adjust that plaster on them, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, so that it does not come undone until it is healed and strengthened the fracture; for many times observed this fracture is strengthened in three weeks.
And if another observed abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in the treatment of them until it is healed, and the abscess be reduced.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters, then you should subtly remove those splinters with what is convenient for the remedy of those injuries.
And if the orifice of the wound is narrow, then you should enlarge it with a fleobotomy according to the quantity of your need.
And then when you have removed those splinters, and not all of them can be removed, then you should close the orifice of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is convenient for it and that adheres to it, until it is healed.
Chapter five.
On the restoration of the hernia when it is strained.
When the intestine is torn from the part in front of the navel, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
When it is torn, or separated, or separated into two parts that do not touch the intestines; and it is easier to restore; or it is such that it touches the intestines, and it is difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is with a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the intestine and follows the torn hernia, and the other stretches out the skin over the affected area.
Then bind the fracture with threads or ties so that it is given the shape of the intestine as it should be, and there is neither protrusion nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place a piece of cloth or wool under the patient's thigh, and its size should be according to the quantity of your need; and stretch and elevate the hernia and tie the cloth around your hand so that you bind the fracture as it should be.
And if you cannot bring the extremity of the furcula to the parts in front, because it has penetrated too far, then it is necessary that the patient be placed upside down on their neck, and a cushion be placed on the middle muscle of their thigh in large size.
And the minister presses the muscle of their thigh to the parts inside until the bone of the furcula is elevated that is in the groin in its own, and then you correct the fracture and bind it with ties on the toes.
More often if you feel stagnations of the furcula and they are inflamed, then it is necessary that you make an incision above that and extract those stagnations to facilitate.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the incision tools that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the furcula the instrument that serves the ciphac; and the instrument is made of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to what you need from it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; that it have two extremities thus as occasions, and one extremity be broad and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound, which you have made when you extracted the sagittal bone is large, and there is a risk of apostema, then adjust the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear apostema, then fill the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the cleft in the wound.
And if apostema occurs, then put a dressing with sharp vinegar and rose oil on it, and apply it to the area.
And thus, that the splint is not removed from the broken bone; and for this reason that you see the patient every day, when the bandage is softened or the splint is seen to be in a bad position, then you correct it and tighten the bandage.
And then stop the patient's sleeping with their neck, and place their pillow under their sleep at night a small cushion; so that when they are elevated by their assistant on that side, then the fracture of the leg is elevated to the elevation of the muscle; or their arm is tied to the neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless there is an accident in the area of the abscess or apostem, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the condition of it to that effect, and adjust the tightness and restore it, and let it be until it is restored and the fracture of the leg is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and some observations in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter six.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula is thrust into the patient's side and does not cut the flesh but only the margins.
And then when the location of the patient is cut — and it is cut in the middle location of the patient — and it is not recognized except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare and reduce it according to its natural shape in every manner possible.
Then apply a poultice made of ground alum and vinegar to the location; and apply a dusting of doubled cloth; and apply to it a subtle plaster of table or board, according to the quantity of the entire spatula or a little more.
But if the plaster forms a hollow in some of the locations of the spatula, then fill that hollow with vinegar and alum, and apply the plaster equally.
And strengthen it with a long bandage from constraint, and with the firmness of the plaster, so that it is not removed from its location.
And examine the dressing every day; and all visits that it is softened, replace another visit and strengthen it and reset the plaster if it is removed from its location.
And lay the patient on their side.
The spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And then when this period is completed, you dissolve the ligament, and be assured that the dissolution of this is not harmful to the bones for which the beginning is not tender nor their dissolution.

Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot, when it is broken in the middle area of it, and this causes pain; but the extremities of it are not moved, except those that are broken and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it, when the middle of it is fractured, it is that it declines to the inner parts and happens to it great pain, and difficulty in walking, and coughing, and vomiting blood; and it is declared in the cavity where the bone is fractured and is not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should be lifted onto their back, and a cushion placed between their shoulders; and press on the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs of it with the hands of two assistants, and proceed subtly in the operation of it and in the adjustment according to all manner that is most possible and that suits it, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
And then pause over the plaster and stop and pause on top of the subtle table of salt or of warm poultice and of their similar lightness, since you envelop that in a cloth; and then subtly apply it to the broken bone so that it is not dislocated; and go with a bandage according to the condition of the back some tightened places, and tighten that one with a strict bandage; and then consider the bandage every hour, and when it is loosened, tighten it.
And if it is necessary to provoke the dissolution of that which needs corrosion or purging, or pain, or abscess that occurs in the area, then touch and divide that and eradicate the plaster and rectify what occurs through that with the art of curing; and then return the plaster, if at times adapted to the face; and adjust to it a strictness until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
It is known that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in coarse locations which are on the back; and of their extremities, from the partition beforehand, nothing occurs to those contrary injuries except through what are cartilaginous. The recognition of the patient is not diminished by the sentiment of their inquiry with them.
And the restoration should be such that it joins the fracture with the fingers according to the possible method, until it is joined as it should be, and then apply a plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel, if necessary for that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the internal divisions, then it causes the patient vehement pain and puncture, similar to the puncture that occurs in one who has pneumonia, and because of this the bone puts forth the wrapping; and it causes difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of bloody mucus; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the medicines are prepared for that with many preparations.
And of them it is said: it is good to give the patient foods that cause inflammation and swelling, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed towards the outer parts.
We would have avoided this because it should not be a light cause to generate a surgical abscess, if it doesn't happen; and if it does happen, then it already helps with that.
And they said some of them that it should be administered with a poultice and then sucked with force; and it seems reasonable, but however it is feared that the poultice will attract the excesses to the place due to the debilitation.
They said some of them: that the place should be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and that powders should be applied between the ribs until it is filled, which should be the binding plaster when you envelop that affected part; and that the patient should be treated with a cure of pleurisy and of food and medicine.
And if the cause violently constricts the patient who does not suffer, and the bone punctures the vein with a sharp pointed instrument, put above the patient, then it is necessary that we make an incision on the place, uncover from the broken rib, and put above that instrument which conserves the diaphragm, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that we introduce the bone with facility, and extract that.
And then adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if there occurs an aposteme (abscess), then cut it and push powders into hot rose oil and apply on the place, and that the patient should be treated with what is suitable for the internal abscess.
And let it be breathed upon the side on which he sleeps, until it is healed.
Chapter nine.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Some times it happens that the fracture affects the neck bones, and similarly their cartilage, and likewise the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be cured or not, then consider this: for if the muscles are already softened and numbed and deadened and cannot be moved or stretched or strengthened, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, there is no feeling of pain, then you should know that it is incurable, and in such conditions it is mortal.
And if you touch them and feel constriction in the fingers and puncture, then you should know that the knot of the bone is saved and that the disease will be cured with treatment.
And if it happens to the vertebrae of the neck similarly and you want to know if it will be cured or not, then consider the weight of the person.
Then if you see that it is already softened, the same thing happens to them that happened to the others, and also when they lie on their back, they have bloating and evacuation against their will, and sometimes it is retained above their back and they want to urinate but cannot, then you should know that it is mortal, and therefore do not bother with their cure.
More often if no accident occurs from those things, the cause is better and the injury is lighter.
And the treatment of it is that you perforate with a needle to the seat of the postulant abscess, which you place on the inflamed spine or on the mangled and rubbed sole, or in the hollows of the shoulders; place it on that thing for several times a day until the abscess is seated.
Then place one of the comforting and exciting plasters, and stretch it over that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the affected side where they feel pain until they are healed.
And if it happens that the bone is dislocated or any cause that has already been separated from it, then you must cut on the tendon and show that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then cure it with a plaster for the unctions of the ulcers, until it is healed.
More often if the skin of the bone is cracked, and it is the end of the wound, then you must put your right thumb in the patient's bottom, and join the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible for a second adjustment; and then place on it a plaster and bandage, if needed, and then bind that.
And if you feel shattered nerves in it, then apply a poultice on that, and remove that, and cure the wound with the treatment that is preceded by the fact that it will be healed.

Of restoration of the narrow passage or Of the restoration of the strait/ channel
The bones of the narrow passages are broken, and if they break, then the fracture of them is not but a communication in the extremities of those and are cut in length and decline to the parts inside; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over that until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is in the extremity of it only, then you splint it according to what is appropriate for equalization until it is assembled into its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the hip or what already declines to the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their belly so that it is prepared for you to restore that fracture. And then when you have splinted it, put a plaster on it, and apply a bandage of cloth or of cotton, and bind tightly so that there is no danger of fracture puncture nor movement of the splint.
Engage the cavity with what fills that space so that it takes the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their side or over their left side.

And if it happens in the seventh bones or they are broken, then pull them out.
Chapter eleven.

Chapter twelve.
On the restoration of arm fractures.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forks; of which one is small, and it is the one that is commonly called the elbow, and the other is large and it is the one that is placed above the small one, from the upper part.
And it happens that only the larger fork is broken, or the smaller one, and sometimes they are both broken together.
And when the smaller fork below is broken, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even easier.
And when the fork of the upper arm is broken, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller fork below, then it is convenient for the doctor to make a small extension with ease so that it can be easily straightened.
More often the larger intestine is what is torn, then it is necessary that you apply extension with great force.
And if both intestines are torn together, then it is necessary that you apply the most intense traction with greatest strength.
And it is necessary that you place the figure of the hand for restoration of it and extension of the stretched-out part on a cushion, and let the palm of the hand be at the divisions above, more than all the other fingers, and the said finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be seated on a low chair.
And let there be a cushion in front of it in its height or depth, so that the patient does not lean forward.
And then the minister extends the arm of the affected part more towards the left hand or to the right, and another minister extends above in a similar manner.
And then the doctor reduces the bone within so that it returns to the best position that it can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are fragments, then you must drill to remove all the pieces from their place with your instrument.
And if it appears that the fragment is separated and touches the tendon and is not part of your hope in the restoration of it, then you sew over it and leave it according to the method that we have spoken of above.
More often if the fracture is on the plague, you have already done a single chapter, and then take the cure of that.
And if there is an abscessed tumor at the beginning of your restoration, then apply a cloth with wax made from olive oil and white wax, and let it be between speculation and subtlety, and tighten the cloth over it with subtle strictness until the abscess breaks; and then remove the wax and apply a plaster prepared from powder of rounded clay and the clarity of dove's dung, and apply bandages; and let the bandage be that which is placed on that fracture, both soft and strong.
And you should know that the name of the bandages of the arm is seen in the major preparations, or whether the fracture is in one splint or in two splints together.
And then apply your tightness over the place of the fracture strongly and vehemently, and when it is more necessary to constrict the parts separated from the fracture or the lower parts of it, apply the constriction a little softer according as the remembrance of it precedes in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and humid, and not harsh or rough, and the thread with which it is tightened should be moderately between subtle and coarse, just as we have recommended.
Examine the limb and the ligament closely for several days. And if a cause arises that requires correction, such as swelling that occurs in the limb, then wrap the limb with hot water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then apply the bandage again.
And if the fracture is already softened and the bone is already reconnected and resembles its original shape, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider more so if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, then wrap it so as to soften it a little and leave it for several days until it heals, and then tighten it.
More so if to the patient no cause of those that we have mentioned occurs, then it is not necessary that it be untied for about twenty days or around that.
And the hand of the patient should be suspended by its virtue of restless movement, and make it sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the disposition of the complications and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter thirteen.
On the restoration of fractures of the thigh and of the fingers.
It is known that to the thigh and to the joints of the fingers a painful fracture occurs, and it does not happen to them but rather an injured wound of visible swellings.

Chapter fourteen.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone, which is called broken and declared by the sensation that it is turned to the front part above and to the worst below.

Chapter 15.

Chapter sixteen.

Chapter three.
On the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When there is a complicated dislocation of one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are displaced from their place, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.

Therefore, that one is already dislocated to the back parts and is called spondylolisthesis or more likely  it is named gibbosity.
But the cure of it is that first consider if the gibbosity occurs from youth, because in that case there is neither cure nor complete healing.
But in that which happens by fall or by shock or similar to these, the Ancients already said the manners of cure with sermon being prolix, from which the most returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated it for the sake of brevity, due to the proximity to my intention, to which I have explained and formed from diverse instruments, with which I will show and expand.
And of those, none are cured except the one that occurs on the back itself with what I have recommended.
And it is that the patient should be laid out on their face on a high bench near a wall, and a stool should be placed under them to support them so that their foot is not hanging.
And then a wooden stick is placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the bench near their head, and another stick in the air holds their feet at the other end of the bench.
And one assistant holds the stick, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another assistant holds the other according to that method.
And then wrap around the head of the patient and underneath the sheet is tied firmly; and extend the end of the sheet to the stick which is near their head, and tie it to them; and then bind the other sheet around their hips and thighs; and behind their legs; and then adjust all the sheets and tie them to the other stick which is near their feet.
And then extend the minister's hand to the ligament at the part of the thigh, and do not move the two thighs and the ligaments of the nearby places where they are fixed, but rather hold them firmly as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine for strength until it returns, or he places on that one table, and then adjusts on the table the nearby feet until they return.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a table whose width or length is about three arms, and carve on the part of which we have said that is near the patient a place in which the extremity of the table. And then place the edge of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other extremity, and stretch tightly until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
And if you want, take the turn which is turned around with the hand; and it is that you fix on the ground at the back of the patient two feet, the length of which is one arm, and fix the other end of it also two feet; and let the length between each foot be a palm, and already made in each foot a hole through which the turn turns, and each one is held firmly on the ground so that it does not move.
And let there be a wooden pole in which the turn is made, and let it be wrapped around the two trunks of the two poles.
And at the extremity of it is a trunk in which the pole is formed, the length of which is one palm and that which is wrapped around; and on the other two poles is similar to that one.
And then tighten the ligaments that you have tied on the chest of the patient in the pole which is near the head of it, and the ligaments that you have tied on the thighs of it in the turn which is near the feet of it.
And then behind each turn there should be a servant who will turn it with their hand, and the doctor will anoint the spine as we have said.
And then after the spine is returned and the area is anointed, then take a plaster made with egg albumen, and apply it; and put it on the plaster of the table from which the width is three fingers or about that, and the length of it is the quantity that takes the place of the swelling, and on the part of the spine above.
And then let it be tied with a ligament as we have said, and give the patient subtle nourishment so that they may be healed.
And if there is some remaining weakness in the area at the end of the treatment, then it is customary that medicines which soften and loosen be administered for a long time, which we have recommended; and already administered in that place a plaster of lead.
And some times accidents occur in the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back, and it is suspected that it is due to that dislocation; and it is that bone which has already been weakened so that it is not advisable to administer this medicine to him, for it will bring about a rapid death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
It is known that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not only cause dislocation, but also do not occur with the same small movement and cavity that occurs in the other joints.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it can be dislocated upwards, or downwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And mostly it is dislocated upwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And the sign of dislocation upwards is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy side to the sick side, it appears longer and the knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the sick leg cannot bend towards the thigh, and it is the place where the thigh is separated by a swelling of hot cauterization, so that the head of the hip has already come out.
In the case of the person in whom dislocation occurs at the joints, it is that the accidents of this are contrary to this accident.
In the case of the person in whom dislocation occurs at the teeth, it is because the leg or thigh is expanded according to its complement, but it does not double that pain which is in the tooth, and if it perforates to go and cannot above that to the teeth; and the urine of him is retained and his anus is swollen, and you must watch the walking of him on the sole.
In the case of the person in whom dislocation occurs at the back, it is that he does not expand the tooth, nor can he double that which doubles his anus, and the leg of him is thus shorter than the other, and the anus is softened, and the head of the thigh is in that place.
But reduction of the species of this dislocation is what you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it has already become accustomed to having it, you administer reduction to it and ointment according to its disposition, then it is not entirely in this cure; and therefore it is not suitable that a prognosis be made in this case.
In that case where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you should reduce it by returning the joint to its proper place and extending it to the inner and outer parts, and moving it to the right and left, for it will strongly return and not require another cure.
But if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare a strong minister who extends the patient's leg from the affected part, or with their hands, or with a bandage that is tied over the patient's knee; and another minister should extend the hand of the one behind to place their hands under the patient's buttocks; and you should hold the linen binding firmly over the knee, and have another minister hold the extremity of the binding.
And it should be extended from the inner part or from the front to the back of the hip, or from the back to the buttocks.
And the extension of all those things should be in a way that the patient is raised from the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is common to all four.
And so if the dislocation returns to what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary to apply the specific treatment that I will speak of regarding its own cure.
In the reduction of the displaced joint, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is in the inner parts, make sure that the patient is placed on their back; and place a pause to the ligaments at the base of the thigh in the location where between the head of the thigh and the spot where the hamstring is found; and then stretch the ligament upwards from the part of the hip to the uppermost part of the buttock.
And then another strong minister should take the patient's arms, and press the thick part of the thigh, and stretch it to the rear parts with violent extension, so that it returns to its own place.
And this species is of the easiest cure among those in which this member is reduced, more so than the other species.
But if you are excused from this and do not obey the treatment with this species of cure at all, then it is necessary that both feet of the patient be tied together with a strong ligament, lightly, on both ankles and both toes.
And let the length of each one be four fingers, and the patient's leg be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And then let the patient be suspended from the top of the beam, and it should be on a mattress; and the length from the ground should be two arms.
And then command a strong youth to gently press the head of the thigh, and let another youth be suspended with the patient, and the other youth pressing the thigh strongly from below, so that the joint returns to its place with speed.
More in the proper reduction, when there is dislocation to the outward part, it is good that the patient puts on a poultice as we have recommended in having coldness; and let the ligament be strengthened over the sick thigh properly and over their chest.
And then let two cloths be placed, one at their feet, and the other at the head; and let adjusted cloths be placed in the middle of the poultice made on which humid draps are wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged by what the cloth is between their thighs, because the extension to the outward parts should not be attracted.
And then let each minister extend to their part, and the doctor with their hand guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then cover so that the leg is gently extended, and it is according to this medical narration on pus; and the doctor puts the palm of their right hand on the patient's languor, and returns the heart to the other hand, and with that tightening stops the extension of the front parts and the part of the teeth.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then do not cover so that the patient's leg is extended to the back parts, and it is raised from the ground; instead, cover that it is placed on a hard surface, just as we would do if their hip is dislocated to the front parts, according to what we have said about placing them on two hips; and it is on their face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is no error.
And cover that compression be administered with a board on the place where the joint is.
Then, as the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and that is you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those groins and the patient stretches their leg and expands its six articulations, then you know that the member is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two hips and put a plaster cast and stretch with a bandage so that the hip is not displaced in any of the parts; and keep the patient at rest for three days or four.
Then return the ligament and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
When, if you see those swellings in the garment, then you know that the dislocation has already been treated, and therefore let the patient go to the bathroom.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you return and plaster it, and stretch it according to your first instruction, and let it be for three days.
Then undo that, and make him delay walking on crutches for some days, until he is comforted.
Chapter thirty-two.
On the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated forward, and backward, and to the sides more than the front parts; and the front parts are not dislocated.
And the sign of its dislocation is that it commands the patient to put their leg on their thigh; then you know that the ankle is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its ways is that you make the patient do this: lift their leg up, so that they extend their leg if possible; place their two hands on their face; and hold their foot between your two feet; then make the ankle adhere to your palms, and press those together over the ankle with force, and another servant lifts their foot until the ankle returns to its place.
The sign of recovery in him is that his leg adheres to the thigh with a light non-working movement.
Then apply a plaster to that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then bind those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then remove it.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until he is healed and comforted.
More so if the reduction of it to you is excessive, then administer a strong extension with ligaments which in the cure of the hip the preceding narration has described.
Chapter 33.
On the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle sometimes is injured by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated according to the injury, and the dislocation of it is caused either by internal or external parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle is swollen due to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of its sprain is from gentle reduction, and it is that it should be extended with ease and held steady until it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to the injury, it is necessary that you make the patient straight, and have a strong and powerful servant behind them in the middle of their back.
And then put your right hand on the upper part of the foot and your left hand on the lower part of the foot in the area of the calf.
And then pull the foot towards your right hand, and with your left hand hold for two fingers, and stretch with your left hand; and press the chest of the foot with your right hand above the leg to relieve the effort.
And do this twice according to what we have recounted.
And then press the chest of the foot on the leg for the third time, and you are at the calf.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two times according to this method, and if the foot is restored to health, it's good; and if not, then repeat the operation, because that will restore it.
But if the reduction of the limb is prohibited to you that we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and stretch a piece of cloth firmly on the ground, and let it catch between their thighs, and you have enveloped them in a sheet so that the disease does not escape.
And then one minister holds their thighs, and another minister stretches the foot with their hands or with a bandage that lies on the ankle of the foot; and then each minister stretches against the extension of their partner, and the cloth is stretched between the patient's thighs, retaining it, so that the body of the patient is not pulled towards the lower parts above their extension.
And then the doctor should adjust the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant should hold the healthy thigh to the patient's sides, so that the dislocation returns to its proper place.
And so when the dislocation returns and it is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then apply a plaster and extract that with a strong bandage, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And beware that you protect the nerves which are located behind the ankle, so that the bandage on that area is not violent and tight, so as to constrict it strongly.
And then leave it like that for two days or for three; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
And then undo it on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if it is forgotten to remove the dressing from that place, it is not done safely that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted on it, and then that which does not receive treatment.
And if a hot abscess occurs to it, then beware that you administer in the evacuation of it that which we previously mentioned, in another place of treatment and of application, until it is reduced.
Chapter four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
It is advisable that one who is dislocated should be treated with a gentle extension of the limb; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then it is advisable to make the patient place their foot on the ground or on a table, and let them stand like that as if they were walking.
And then put your foot on theirs, and press their foot on what seems to be the joint, and press down with your foot in an upright manner until it returns, and you will see that it has been properly treated and does not appear out of place.
And then put a table under the belly of their foot that holds the entire foot, which has two ends, and tie it with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then undo it and make them rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and the recovery is assured.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocations that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with several things at the same time.
When accidents occur due to certain causes, and you are piercing through the treatment of that and in the restoration of it, many seen successes lead to death.
And for this it is not suitable that anyone presumes over the treatment of it, except one who is wise in the art, with long usage, merciful and pitied, slow, not hasty nor presumptuous; and who uses in the commencement of medicines that cause abscesses to be cut out only, and lift the patient to divine judgment, but rather to the one to whom you expect salvation from loss, and from the sickness alleviation, and in which appears some hope. And then strive to remove yourself so far from it the reduction in the commencement of the cause, waiting for an abscess to be cut out.
And then if the member returns as we wish, then administer treatment which will cut out the abscess and cure the wound with what is convenient for it from the ointments of the dried placents.
And if there is a dislocation with a fracture and it occurs in the bones that are separated by joints, then you will pierce through their articulation, and do in this what we have said about simple illnesses of this type that precede in the locations of these.
And beware and avoid according to your ability the one who is in the intestines of fear, as my preaching has preceded you; for that which is better for you, you owe to your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutations to your body, verify those in your time.
